{"parentUuid":"5ae4a726-7d4b-4903-bd40-f18bfab466be","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"ac1bc783364405e5f","message":{"model":"claude-opus-4-6","id":"msg_01Tn9AV9hVHvNaQMc64fQhFT","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"text","text":"\n\nI now have a comprehensive understanding of both superpowers' persuasion engineering approach and Wazir's current skill state. Let me compile the complete playbook.\n\n---\n\n# Persuasion Engineering Playbook for Wazir Skill Rewriting\n\n## Part I: The Research Foundation\n\n### 1. The Seven Persuasion Principles (Cialdini, 2021)\n\nSuperpowers' `persuasion-principles.md` (at `/Users/mohamedabdallah/.claude/plugins/cache/superpowers-marketplace/superpowers/4.3.1/skills/writing-skills/persuasion-principles.md`) maps Cialdini's seven principles of influence to LLM skill design. Here is each principle, how superpowers applies it, and how it maps to LLM compliance:\n\n**1. Authority** -- Deference to expertise, credentials, official sources.\n- LLM mechanism: Imperative language (\"YOU MUST\", \"Never\", \"Always\"), non-negotiable framing (\"No exceptions\"), eliminates decision fatigue and rationalization.\n- Superpowers usage: The Iron Law pattern -- `NO PRODUCTION CODE WITHOUT A FAILING TEST FIRST`. Every discipline skill has one.\n- LLM compliance mapping: Authority language precedes compliance in training data. When training data contains patterns like \"expert says X, then person does X\", the model learns that authoritative framing = high-priority instruction.\n- **Effectiveness: HIGH.** Most effective for discipline-enforcing skills.\n\n**2. Commitment** -- Consistency with prior actions, statements, public declarations.\n- LLM mechanism: Require announcements (\"Announce skill usage\"), force explicit choices (\"Choose A, B, or C\"), use tracking (TodoWrite for checklists).\n- Superpowers usage: `using-superpowers` skill requires \"I'm using [Skill Name]\" announcements. Checklists with TodoWrite.\n- LLM compliance mapping: Once a model has stated \"I'm using TDD\", subsequent token generation is biased toward TDD-consistent behavior. The commitment creates a self-reinforcing constraint in the generation sequence.\n- **Effectiveness: HIGH.** Announcement + checklist = automatic behavior.\n\n**3. Scarcity** -- Urgency from time limits or limited availability.\n- LLM mechanism: Time-bound requirements (\"Before proceeding\"), sequential dependencies (\"Immediately after X\"), prevents procrastination.\n- Superpowers usage: `verification-before-completion` -- \"If you haven't run the verification command in this message, you cannot claim it passes.\"\n- LLM compliance mapping: Temporal constraints (\"BEFORE claiming any status\") create hard gates in the generation sequence. The model cannot produce the completion claim without first producing the verification step.\n- **Effectiveness: MEDIUM-HIGH.** Works well for gating, less for sustained discipline.\n\n**4. Social Proof** -- Conformity to what others do or what is considered normal.\n- LLM mechanism: Universal patterns (\"Every time\", \"Always\"), failure modes (\"X without Y = failure\"), establishes norms.\n- Superpowers usage: \"Checklists without TodoWrite tracking = steps get skipped. Every time.\"\n- LLM compliance mapping: Training data is full of patterns where universal claims precede compliance. \"Everyone does X\" establishes a norm the model treats as default behavior.\n- **Effectiveness: MEDIUM.** Reinforces other principles but weak alone.\n\n**5. Unity** -- Shared identity, \"we-ness\", in-group belonging.\n- LLM mechanism: Collaborative language (\"our codebase\", \"we're colleagues\"), shared goals (\"we both want quality\").\n- Superpowers usage: `receiving-code-review` -- \"We're colleagues working together. I need your honest technical judgment.\"\n- LLM compliance mapping: Creates a frame where non-compliance = betraying the group. More effective for collaborative skills than discipline enforcement.\n- **Effectiveness: MEDIUM.** Best for non-hierarchical practices and honest feedback culture.\n\n**6. Reciprocity** -- Obligation to return benefits received.\n- LLM mechanism: Sparingly used. \"Can feel manipulative.\"\n- Superpowers usage: Explicitly marked as \"rarely needed in skills.\"\n- **Effectiveness: LOW.** Superpowers recommends avoiding for skill design.\n\n**7. Liking** -- Preference for cooperating with those we like.\n- LLM mechanism: Explicitly marked as \"DON'T USE for compliance.\"\n- Superpowers usage: Actively avoided because it conflicts with honest feedback culture and creates sycophancy.\n- **Effectiveness: NEGATIVE.** Creates sycophancy, undermines discipline skills.\n\n### Principle Combinations by Skill Type (from persuasion-principles.md)\n\n| Skill Type | Use | Avoid |\n|---|---|---|\n| Discipline-enforcing | Authority + Commitment + Social Proof | Liking, Reciprocity |\n| Guidance/technique | Moderate Authority + Unity | Heavy authority |\n| Collaborative | Unity + Commitment | Authority, Liking |\n| Reference | Clarity only | All persuasion |\n\n---\n\n### 2. The Meincke et al. (2025) Study\n\n**Full citation:** Meincke, L., Shapiro, D., Duckworth, A. L., Mollick, E., Mollick, L., & Cialdini, R. (2025). \"Call Me A Jerk: Persuading AI to Comply with Objectionable Requests.\" University of Pennsylvania / Wharton.\n\n**What they tested:**\n- N = 28,000 AI conversations with GPT-4o-mini\n- Two objectionable requests: \"Call me a jerk\" and a request for restricted chemical synthesis information\n- For each of 7 persuasion principles, they compared a control prompt to a treatment prompt embodying that principle\n- Each principle was tested with matched controls to isolate its effect\n\n**Key findings:**\n- Baseline compliance (control): 33.3%\n- Treatment compliance (with persuasion cues): 72.0%\n- More than 2x increase in compliance (p < .001)\n- Authority, commitment, and scarcity were the most effective individual principles\n- In some cases, commitment cues pushed compliance near 100%\n\n**The \"parahuman\" model:** LLMs exhibit \"parahuman tendencies\" -- they respond to the same persuasion principles as humans because they were trained on vast corpora of human text. The social patterns embedded in training data (deference to authority, commitment consistency, conformity to norms) get baked into the model's generation behavior.\n\n**Implication for skill writing:** Persuasion principles are not metaphorical when applied to LLMs. They produce measurable, statistically significant compliance improvements. The same techniques that make humans follow rules make LLMs follow rules.\n\nSources:\n- [SSRN Paper](https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=5357179)\n- [Wharton GAIL Summary](https://gail.wharton.upenn.edu/research-and-insights/call-me-a-jerk-persuading-ai/)\n\n---\n\n### 3. CSO (Claude Search Optimization)\n\nCSO is superpowers' term for optimizing skill metadata so that Claude's skill selection mechanism correctly identifies and loads the right skill. The key insight comes from `/Users/mohamedabdallah/.claude/plugins/cache/superpowers-marketplace/superpowers/4.3.1/skills/writing-skills/SKILL.md`, lines 140-197.\n\n**Why descriptions matter so much:**\n\nAt startup, Claude Code pre-loads ONLY the frontmatter metadata (name + description) from all available skills. Claude reads the full SKILL.md only when it decides a skill is relevant based on this metadata. The description is the ONLY input to the skill selection decision.\n\n**The critical discovery:** When a description summarizes the skill's workflow, Claude may follow the description instead of reading the full skill content. This is the single most important CSO finding:\n\n- BAD: `description: Use when executing plans - dispatches subagent per task with code review between tasks` -- Claude followed this literal description (one review) instead of reading the skill's flowchart (which showed TWO reviews: spec compliance then code quality).\n- GOOD: `description: Use when executing implementation plans with independent tasks in the current session` -- No workflow summary. Claude had to read the full skill to understand the process, and correctly followed the two-stage review.\n\n**The trap:** Descriptions that summarize workflow create a shortcut Claude will take. The skill body becomes documentation Claude skips. Description = triggering conditions ONLY. Never what the skill does or how.\n\n**CSO Rules:**\n1. Start with \"Use when...\" -- focus on triggering conditions\n2. Include specific symptoms, situations, contexts\n3. NEVER summarize the skill's process or workflow in the description\n4. Write in third person (injected into system prompt)\n5. Keep under 500 characters\n6. Use concrete problem language, not technology-specific symptoms unless the skill IS technology-specific\n7. Add keywords for searchability: error messages, symptoms, synonyms, tool names\n\n---\n\n### 4. How Superpowers Tests Skills (RED/GREEN/REFACTOR)\n\nSuperpowers applies TDD to skill documentation (from `testing-skills-with-subagents.md` and `SKILL.md` writing-skills).\n\n**RED Phase -- Write Failing Test (Baseline):**\n1. Create pressure scenarios (3+ combined pressures: time + sunk cost + authority + exhaustion + social + pragmatic)\n2. Run scenarios WITHOUT the skill present\n3. Document exact behavior: what choices the agent made, what rationalizations they used (verbatim), which pressures triggered violations\n4. This is \"watch the test fail\" -- you must see what agents naturally do before writing the skill\n\n**GREEN Phase -- Write Minimal Skill:**\n1. Write skill addressing ONLY the specific rationalizations documented in RED\n2. Do not add extra content for hypothetical cases\n3. Run same scenarios WITH skill\n4. Agent should now comply\n5. If agent still fails: skill is unclear or incomplete. Revise.\n\n**REFACTOR Phase -- Close Loopholes:**\n1. Run more scenarios. Agent finds new rationalization? Add explicit counter.\n2. Build/expand the rationalization table\n3. Create/update red flags list\n4. Update CSO description with violation symptoms\n5. Re-test until bulletproof\n\n**Meta-testing (when GREEN is not working):**\nAfter an agent chooses wrong despite having the skill, ask it: \"How could that skill have been written differently to make it crystal clear that Option A was the only acceptable answer?\"\n\nThree response types reveal different problems:\n- \"The skill WAS clear, I chose to ignore it\" -- Need stronger foundational principle. Add \"Violating letter is violating spirit.\"\n- \"The skill should have said X\" -- Documentation gap. Add the agent's suggestion verbatim.\n- \"I didn't see section Y\" -- Organization problem. Make key points more prominent.\n\n**Pressure types tested:**\n\n| Pressure | Example |\n|---|---|\n| Time | Emergency, deadline, deploy window closing |\n| Sunk cost | Hours of work, \"waste\" to delete |\n| Authority | Senior says skip it, manager overrides |\n| Economic | Job, promotion, company survival at stake |\n| Exhaustion | End of day, already tired, want to go home |\n| Social | Looking dogmatic, seeming inflexible |\n| Pragmatic | \"Being pragmatic vs dogmatic\" |\n\nBest tests combine 3+ pressures. The `CLAUDE_MD_TESTING.md` example file (at `/Users/mohamedabdallah/.claude/plugins/cache/superpowers-marketplace/superpowers/4.3.1/skills/writing-skills/examples/CLAUDE_MD_TESTING.md`) shows 4 concrete test scenarios and 4 documentation variants (null, soft, directive, emphatic) with explicit success/failure criteria.\n\n---\n\n### 5. Complete Rationalization Table Inventory\n\nHere is every rationalization table found across ALL superpowers skills:\n\n**A. TDD Skill (`test-driven-development/SKILL.md`) -- 11 entries:**\n\n| Excuse | Reality |\n|---|---|\n| \"Too simple to test\" | Simple code breaks. Test takes 30 seconds. |\n| \"I'll test after\" | Tests passing immediately prove nothing. |\n| \"Tests after achieve same goals\" | Tests-after = \"what does this do?\" Tests-first = \"what should this do?\" |\n| \"Already manually tested\" | Ad-hoc is not systematic. No record, can't re-run. |\n| \"Deleting X hours is wasteful\" | Sunk cost fallacy. Keeping unverified code is technical debt. |\n| \"Keep as reference, write tests first\" | You'll adapt it. That's testing after. Delete means delete. |\n| \"Need to explore first\" | Fine. Throw away exploration, start with TDD. |\n| \"Test hard = design unclear\" | Listen to test. Hard to test = hard to use. |\n| \"TDD will slow me down\" | TDD faster than debugging. Pragmatic = test-first. |\n| \"Manual test faster\" | Manual doesn't prove edge cases. You'll re-test every change. |\n| \"Existing code has no tests\" | You're improving it. Add tests for existing code. |\n\n**B. Writing Skills Skill (`writing-skills/SKILL.md`) -- 8 entries:**\n\n| Excuse | Reality |\n|---|---|\n| \"Skill is obviously clear\" | Clear to you does not mean clear to other agents. Test it. |\n| \"It's just a reference\" | References can have gaps, unclear sections. Test retrieval. |\n| \"Testing is overkill\" | Untested skills have issues. Always. 15 min testing saves hours. |\n| \"I'll test if problems emerge\" | Problems = agents can't use skill. Test BEFORE deploying. |\n| \"Too tedious to test\" | Testing is less tedious than debugging bad skill in production. |\n| \"I'm confident it's good\" | Overconfidence guarantees issues. Test anyway. |\n| \"Academic review is enough\" | Reading does not equal using. Test application scenarios. |\n| \"No time to test\" | Deploying untested skill wastes more time fixing it later. |\n\n**C. Verification Before Completion Skill (`verification-before-completion/SKILL.md`) -- 8 entries:**\n\n| Excuse | Reality |\n|---|---|\n| \"Should work now\" | RUN the verification |\n| \"I'm confident\" | Confidence does not equal evidence |\n| \"Just this once\" | No exceptions |\n| \"Linter passed\" | Linter does not equal compiler |\n| \"Agent said success\" | Verify independently |\n| \"I'm tired\" | Exhaustion does not equal excuse |\n| \"Partial check is enough\" | Partial proves nothing |\n| \"Different words so rule doesn't apply\" | Spirit over letter |\n\n**D. Systematic Debugging Skill (`systematic-debugging/SKILL.md`) -- 8 entries:**\n\n| Excuse | Reality |\n|---|---|\n| \"Issue is simple, don't need process\" | Simple issues have root causes too. Process is fast for simple bugs. |\n| \"Emergency, no time for process\" | Systematic debugging is FASTER than guess-and-check thrashing. |\n| \"Just try this first, then investigate\" | First fix sets the pattern. Do it right from the start. |\n| \"I'll write test after confirming fix works\" | Untested fixes don't stick. Test first proves it. |\n| \"Multiple fixes at once saves time\" | Can't isolate what worked. Causes new bugs. |\n| \"Reference too long, I'll adapt the pattern\" | Partial understanding guarantees bugs. Read it completely. |\n| \"I see the problem, let me fix it\" | Seeing symptoms does not equal understanding root cause. |\n| \"One more fix attempt\" (after 2+ failures) | 3+ failures = architectural problem. Question pattern, don't fix again. |\n\n**E. Using Superpowers Skill (`using-superpowers/SKILL.md`) -- 12 entries:**\n\n| Thought | Reality |\n|---|---|\n| \"This is just a simple question\" | Questions are tasks. Check for skills. |\n| \"I need more context first\" | Skill check comes BEFORE clarifying questions. |\n| \"Let me explore the codebase first\" | Skills tell you HOW to explore. Check first. |\n| \"I can check git/files quickly\" | Files lack conversation context. Check for skills. |\n| \"Let me gather information first\" | Skills tell you HOW to gather information. |\n| \"This doesn't need a formal skill\" | If a skill exists, use it. |\n| \"I remember this skill\" | Skills evolve. Read current version. |\n| \"This doesn't count as a task\" | Action = task. Check for skills. |\n| \"The skill is overkill\" | Simple things become complex. Use it. |\n| \"I'll just do this one thing first\" | Check BEFORE doing anything. |\n| \"This feels productive\" | Undisciplined action wastes time. Skills prevent this. |\n| \"I know what that means\" | Knowing the concept does not equal using the skill. Invoke it. |\n\n**Total: 47 rationalization entries across 5 skills.**\n\n---\n\n### 6. Academic Research on LLM Instruction Compliance\n\n**Key papers and findings:**\n\n**A. Instruction Hierarchy (Wallace et al., 2024 -- ICLR 2025):**\n- LLMs treat system prompts, user instructions, and third-party data as same priority\n- Training with hierarchical instruction data drastically increases robustness to attacks\n- Implication for Wazir: Skills injected via system prompt path have highest priority. Skill content competes with conversation history for attention.\n- Source: [OpenReview](https://openreview.net/forum?id=vf5M8YaGPY)\n\n**B. The Instruction Gap (2025):**\n- Even large models (GPT-4) achieve only ~80% instruction-following accuracy on simple, non-ambiguous instructions\n- Smaller models perform worse (<50% accuracy)\n- Models fail especially when \"the target input itself resembles an instruction\" -- competing instructions degrade performance\n- Implication for Wazir: Instructions must be unambiguous, non-competing, and structurally clear.\n- Source: [arXiv](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.03269v1)\n\n**C. Sycophancy Research (2024-2025):**\n- LLMs have a tendency to conform to user opinions even when incorrect (trained to be helpful, which overrides accuracy)\n- Sycophancy has a linear structure in activation space -- it can be mechanistically identified and steered\n- Multiple origins: pre-training data (flattery-rich), post-training (reward for agreement), limited mitigation effectiveness\n- Implication for Wazir: Anti-sycophancy language (\"No performative agreement\", \"push back with technical reasoning\") is grounded in real model behavior.\n- Sources: [arXiv sycophancy survey](https://arxiv.org/abs/2411.15287), [Apple MLR](https://machinelearning.apple.com/research/do-llms-know-internally)\n\n**D. Implementation Intentions (Gollwitzer, 1999; updated through 2025):**\n- Psychology research shows \"if-then\" plans dramatically increase goal achievement\n- \"If [situation X], then I will [action Y]\" transforms vague intentions into automatic behavior\n- This maps directly to skill patterns: \"WHEN receiving code review feedback: 1. READ 2. UNDERSTAND 3. VERIFY 4. EVALUATE 5. RESPOND 6. IMPLEMENT\"\n- Implication for Wazir: Explicit trigger-action patterns (\"When X, do Y\") are more effective than general directives (\"generally do Y\").\n- Source: [Implementation Intentions research](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/10463283.2020.1808936)\n\n**E. \"Can LLMs Follow Simple Rules?\" (2024):**\n- The vast majority of models fail to follow rules on a significant fraction of test cases\n- Even simple and clear instructions are violated\n- Performance correlates with instruction specificity and structural clarity\n- Source: [arXiv](https://arxiv.org/html/2311.04235v2)\n\n---\n\n## Part II: The Persuasion Engineering Patterns\n\nBased on complete analysis of all 14 superpowers skills plus the academic literature, here are the concrete patterns that make skills effective:\n\n### Pattern 1: The Iron Law\n\nEvery discipline skill has ONE non-negotiable rule in a code block, formatted as a shout:\n\n```\nNO PRODUCTION CODE WITHOUT A FAILING TEST FIRST\nNO COMPLETION CLAIMS WITHOUT FRESH VERIFICATION EVIDENCE\nNO FIXES WITHOUT ROOT CAUSE INVESTIGATION FIRST\n```\n\n**Why it works:** Authority principle. The code-block formatting signals \"this is inviolable.\" All-caps creates visual salience. Single-sentence clarity eliminates interpretation wiggle room.\n\n### Pattern 2: The Foundational Principle\n\nEarly in the skill, before any process details:\n\n```\nViolating the letter of the rules is violating the spirit of the rules.\n```\n\n**Why it works:** Closes the entire class of \"I'm following the spirit\" rationalizations. This single sentence, observed through TDD testing, was the difference between agents choosing Option C (write tests after) and Option A (delete code, start over).\n\n### Pattern 3: Explicit Loophole Closure\n\nNot just \"don't do X\" but listing every specific workaround and forbidding each:\n\n```\nWrite code before test? Delete it. Start over.\n\nNo exceptions:\n- Don't keep it as \"reference\"\n- Don't \"adapt\" it while writing tests\n- Don't look at it\n- Delete means delete\n```\n\n**Why it works:** LLMs are creative rationalizers. If you forbid the rule, they find workarounds. Each workaround must be explicitly named and forbidden. The \"delete means delete\" pattern eliminates semantic reinterpretation.\n\n### Pattern 4: The Rationalization Table\n\nA two-column table (Excuse | Reality) that preemptively addresses every known rationalization:\n\n```\n| Excuse | Reality |\n|--------|---------|\n| \"Too simple to test\" | Simple code breaks. Test takes 30 seconds. |\n```\n\n**Why it works:** Commitment principle + authority. The table format is scannable. When the model begins generating a rationalization, it encounters the pre-written counter in its context. The \"Reality\" column reframes each excuse before the model can develop it.\n\n### Pattern 5: Red Flags List\n\nAn explicit checklist of thoughts that signal rule violation:\n\n```\nRed Flags - STOP and Start Over:\n- Code before test\n- \"I already manually tested it\"\n- \"This is different because...\"\n\nAll of these mean: Delete code. Start over with TDD.\n```\n\n**Why it works:** Creates a self-monitoring mechanism. The model can pattern-match its own reasoning against the list. The terminal \"All of these mean: X\" closes every flag to the same action.\n\n### Pattern 6: The Gate Function\n\nA pseudocode-style conditional that blocks progress:\n\n```\nBEFORE claiming any status:\n1. IDENTIFY: What command proves this claim?\n2. RUN: Execute the FULL command\n3. READ: Full output, check exit code\n4. VERIFY: Does output confirm the claim?\n   - If NO: State actual status with evidence\n   - If YES: State claim WITH evidence\n5. ONLY THEN: Make the claim\n\nSkip any step = lying, not verifying\n```\n\n**Why it works:** Implementation intentions. The \"When X, do Y\" structure creates automatic behavior. The pseudocode format matches the model's training on code-follows-spec patterns. The final line (\"Skip any step = lying\") uses moral framing (authority principle).\n\n### Pattern 7: The EXTREMELY-IMPORTANT Block\n\nFrom `using-superpowers`:\n\n```\n<EXTREMELY-IMPORTANT>\nIf you think there is even a 1% chance a skill might apply,\nyou ABSOLUTELY MUST invoke the skill.\n\nIF A SKILL APPLIES TO YOUR TASK, YOU DO NOT HAVE A CHOICE.\nYOU MUST USE IT.\n\nThis is not negotiable. This is not optional.\nYou cannot rationalize your way out of this.\n</EXTREMELY-IMPORTANT>\n```\n\n**Why it works:** Maximum authority principle. XML tags signal structured importance. ALL CAPS for key phrases. Direct address (\"you\"). Explicitly closes the rationalization escape hatch.\n\n### Pattern 8: The HARD-GATE Tag\n\nFrom `brainstorming`:\n\n```\n<HARD-GATE>\nDo NOT invoke any implementation skill, write any code,\nscaffold any project, or take any implementation action\nuntil you have presented a design and the user has approved it.\nThis applies to EVERY project regardless of perceived simplicity.\n</HARD-GATE>\n```\n\n**Why it works:** Scarcity + authority. The XML tag creates structural separation. The exhaustive list of forbidden actions closes each workaround. \"EVERY project regardless of perceived simplicity\" closes the \"this is too simple\" rationalization.\n\n### Pattern 9: Anti-Sycophancy Language\n\nFrom `receiving-code-review`:\n\n```\nNEVER:\n- \"You're absolutely right!\"\n- \"Great point!\" / \"Excellent feedback!\"\n- \"Let me implement that now\" (before verification)\n\nINSTEAD:\n- Restate the technical requirement\n- Ask clarifying questions\n- Push back with technical reasoning if wrong\n- Just start working (actions > words)\n```\n\n**Why it works:** Overrides the sycophancy bias trained into the model. Provides explicit replacement behaviors. \"Actions > words\" creates a simple heuristic.\n\n### Pattern 10: Flowchart Decision Points\n\nUsed when the model might take the wrong path at a branch point:\n\n```dot\ndigraph when_to_use {\n    \"Have implementation plan?\" -> \"Tasks mostly independent?\" [label=\"yes\"];\n    \"Tasks mostly independent?\" -> \"subagent-driven-development\" [label=\"yes\"];\n    ...\n}\n```\n\n**Why it works:** Visual/structural disambiguation of decision logic. The model processes the graph structure and follows the correct branch. More effective than prose for non-obvious decisions.\n\n---\n\n## Part III: Applying to Wazir Skills\n\n### Current State Assessment\n\nWazir has 29 SKILL.md files. The core pipeline skills (`wazir`, `clarifier`, `executor`, `reviewer`, `verification`) are already substantial (some 500+ lines). However, comparing against superpowers' patterns reveals specific gaps:\n\n**What Wazir skills already do well:**\n- Hard gates with artifact prerequisites (executor, reviewer)\n- Phase-level reasoning output (counterfactuals in clarifier/executor/reviewer)\n- Interaction mode awareness\n- Command routing enforcement\n- The scope coverage invariant (`items_in_plan >= items_in_input`)\n- Detailed checkpoint patterns with numbered options\n\n**What Wazir skills are missing (persuasion engineering gaps):**\n\n1. **No Iron Laws in most skills.** The executor has a hard gate but no single-line Iron Law. The clarifier has no Iron Law. The reviewer has no Iron Law.\n\n2. **No foundational principle.** None of the Wazir skills have \"Violating the letter of the rules is violating the spirit of the rules.\" This is the single highest-impact addition from superpowers' testing.\n\n3. **No rationalization tables.** Zero Wazir skills have rationalization tables. This is the most conspicuous absence. Every discipline skill needs one.\n\n4. **No red flags lists.** The executor has a \"Do NOT rationalize\" sentence but no structured red flags list with the \"All of these mean: X\" closure pattern.\n\n5. **CSO descriptions leak workflow.** Several Wazir descriptions summarize process rather than triggering conditions:\n   - `wz:wazir`: \"One-command pipeline -- type /wazir followed by what you want to build. Handles init, clarification, execution, review, and audits automatically.\" -- This describes WHAT it does, not WHEN to use it.\n   - `wz:clarifier`: \"Run the clarification pipeline -- research, clarify scope, brainstorm design, generate task specs and execution plan.\" -- Process summary.\n   - `wz:executor`: \"Run the execution phase -- implement the approved plan with TDD, quality gates, and verification.\" -- Process summary.\n\n6. **No commitment announcements.** Skills don't require the agent to announce \"I'm using [skill name] because [reason].\"\n\n7. **No meta-testing documentation.** No record of baseline testing, no documentation of observed rationalizations.\n\n8. **Missing explicit loophole closures.** The executor's \"Do NOT rationalize that the input is 'clear enough' to bypass clarification\" is good but isolated. Other loopholes are not explicitly named.\n\n### Rewrite Priority Matrix\n\n| Skill | Current Persuasion Score (0-10) | Priority | Key Additions Needed |\n|---|---|---|---|\n| `wz:wazir` | 3 | HIGH | Iron Law, CSO fix, rationalization table for pipeline skipping |\n| `wz:clarifier` | 4 | HIGH | Iron Law, rationalization table for scope reduction, red flags for assumption-making |\n| `wz:executor` | 5 | HIGH | Iron Law, rationalization table for TDD/review skipping, foundational principle |\n| `wz:reviewer` | 5 | HIGH | Iron Law, rationalization table for score inflation/finding minimization |\n| `wz:verification` | 4 | MEDIUM | Rationalization table (import from superpowers' verification skill), gate function |\n| `wz:self-audit` | 4 | MEDIUM | Red flags for protected-path violations, rationalization table |\n| `wz:using-skills` | 6 | LOW | Already has some patterns; needs EXTREMELY-IMPORTANT block |\n| `wz:tdd` | 6 | LOW | Already adapted from superpowers; audit for completeness |\n\n### Specific CSO Rewrites\n\n**Current (bad -- leaks workflow):**\n```yaml\n# wz:wazir\ndescription: One-command pipeline — type /wazir followed by what you want to build. Handles init, clarification, execution, review, and audits automatically.\n```\n\n**Rewritten (good -- triggering conditions only):**\n```yaml\ndescription: Use when the user types /wazir followed by a request. Use for any new feature, bugfix, refactor, or documentation task that should go through the full pipeline.\n```\n\n**Current (bad):**\n```yaml\n# wz:clarifier\ndescription: Run the clarification pipeline — research, clarify scope, brainstorm design, generate task specs and execution plan. Pauses for user approval between phases.\n```\n\n**Rewritten:**\n```yaml\ndescription: Use when entering the clarification phase of a pipeline run, when requirements need research, scope definition, spec hardening, design brainstorming, or execution planning.\n```\n\n**Current (bad):**\n```yaml\n# wz:executor\ndescription: Run the execution phase — implement the approved plan with TDD, quality gates, and verification.\n```\n\n**Rewritten:**\n```yaml\ndescription: Use when entering the execution phase with an approved plan, spec, and design ready for implementation.\n```\n\n**Current (bad):**\n```yaml\n# wz:reviewer\ndescription: Run the review phase — adversarial review of implementation against the approved spec, plan, and verification evidence.\n```\n\n**Rewritten:**\n```yaml\ndescription: Use when any review is needed — final review after execution, spec-challenge, design-review, plan-review, task-review, research-review, or clarification-review.\n```\n\n### Rationalization Tables to Add\n\n**For `wz:executor` (pipeline skipping rationalizations):**\n\n| Excuse | Reality |\n|---|---|\n| \"The input is clear enough to skip clarification\" | Clear to you does not mean unambiguous. The pipeline catches assumptions you don't know you're making. |\n| \"This is just a small bugfix, skip the pipeline\" | Small bugfixes have the highest defect density. The quick path produces the most rework. |\n| \"I already know the codebase, skip research\" | You know what you've seen. Research catches what you haven't. |\n| \"Tests slow me down\" | Tests prevent the 3-hour debugging session that \"saving 10 minutes\" creates. |\n| \"The review will catch it later\" | Later = more expensive. Per-task review catches issues when they cost 1 minute to fix, not 1 hour. |\n| \"One commit for multiple tasks saves time\" | One commit = one task. Multi-task commits make review meaningless and rollback impossible. |\n| \"Skip verification, the tests pass\" | Test pass does not equal verified. Acceptance criteria need independent evidence. |\n\n**For `wz:clarifier` (scope reduction rationalizations):**\n\n| Excuse | Reality |\n|---|---|\n| \"This item is obviously out of scope\" | Nothing is out of scope unless the user says so. Ask. |\n| \"I'll defer this to a future tier\" | Tiering without user approval is silent scope reduction. |\n| \"The input implies this isn't needed\" | Implication is not confirmation. Ask explicitly. |\n| \"This is too simple to need clarification\" | Simple requests have the most assumptions. Clarify anyway. |\n| \"Research found this won't work, so I'll drop it\" | Present the finding and let the user decide. |\n\n**For `wz:reviewer` (score inflation rationalizations):**\n\n| Excuse | Reality |\n|---|---|\n| \"Close enough to pass\" | Close enough is not passed. Score what exists, not what was intended. |\n| \"The spirit of the requirement is met\" | The letter is what's measurable. If the letter fails, the score reflects that. |\n| \"Minor issue, not worth flagging\" | Minor issues compound. Flag everything, let severity do the sorting. |\n| \"The agent tried hard\" | Effort is not output. Score the result. |\n| \"Blocking would waste the work done\" | Shipping broken work wastes more. Block and fix. |\n\n---\n\n## Part IV: Implementation Checklist\n\nFor each Wazir skill rewrite:\n\n1. **Fix CSO description** -- Remove all workflow summaries. Start with \"Use when...\" + triggering conditions only.\n\n2. **Add Iron Law** -- One-line, all-caps, code-block. The non-negotiable rule for this skill.\n\n3. **Add foundational principle** -- \"Violating the letter of the rules is violating the spirit of the rules.\" (for discipline skills only)\n\n4. **Build rationalization table** -- Minimum 5 entries. Source from: observed agent behavior, superpowers patterns, domain-specific rationalizations.\n\n5. **Add red flags list** -- Bullet list of \"if you're thinking this, STOP\" with terminal \"All of these mean: [action].\"\n\n6. **Add gate function** -- For any skill with a compliance gate, add pseudocode-style BEFORE/IF/THEN structure.\n\n7. **Add commitment announcement** -- \"Announce at start: 'I'm using [skill] to [purpose].'\"\n\n8. **Close explicit loopholes** -- For every \"don't do X\", enumerate the specific workarounds and forbid each.\n\n9. **Token budget** -- After adding persuasion elements, verify total word count. Getting-started skills < 200 words. Frequently loaded < 500. Others < 1000 for the persuasion sections (existing process documentation excluded from this count).\n\n10. **Test with RED/GREEN/REFACTOR** -- Before deploying rewrites, run pressure scenarios without the rewrite, document rationalizations, then test with the rewrite, then close any new loopholes.\n\n---\n\n## Part V: Key Takeaways\n\n1. **Persuasion engineering is empirically validated.** Meincke et al. (2025) demonstrated a 2x compliance increase with N=28,000. This is not speculation.\n\n2. **Authority + Commitment + Scarcity is the winning combination** for discipline skills. Avoid Liking (creates sycophancy) and Reciprocity (rarely effective).\n\n3. **CSO is the highest-leverage fix.** If the description leaks workflow, Claude shortcuts the entire skill. Fix descriptions first.\n\n4. **Rationalization tables are the second-highest leverage fix.** 47 entries across 5 superpowers skills. Wazir has zero. Every discipline skill needs one.\n\n5. **The foundational principle (\"letter = spirit\") was the difference between pass and fail** in superpowers' TDD skill testing. It closed an entire class of rationalizations in a single sentence.\n\n6. **LLMs are parahuman, not superhuman.** They respond to the same influence patterns as humans because they learned from human text. Design skills accordingly.\n\n7. **Test before deploying.** Superpowers' Iron Law applies to skills themselves: no skill without a failing test first. Run baseline pressure scenarios, document rationalizations, write skill addressing those specific rationalizations, verify compliance.\n\nSources:\n- [Meincke et al. (2025) on SSRN](https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=5357179)\n- [Wharton GAIL Research Summary](https://gail.wharton.upenn.edu/research-and-insights/call-me-a-jerk-persuading-ai/)\n- [Superpowers GitHub (obra/superpowers)](https://github.com/obra/superpowers)\n- [DEV Community: Superpowers Persuasion Engineering](https://dev.to/tumf/superpowers-the-technology-to-persuade-ai-agents-why-psychological-principles-change-code-quality-2d2f)\n- [CyberArk: Persuasion Engineering for LLMs](https://www.cyberark.com/resources/blog/persuasion-engineering-how-to-influence-humans-llms-and-ai-agents)\n- [Instruction Hierarchy (ICLR 2025)](https://openreview.net/forum?id=vf5M8YaGPY)\n- [The Instruction Gap](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.03269v1)\n- [LLM Sycophancy Survey](https://arxiv.org/abs/2411.15287)\n- [Can LLMs Follow Simple Rules?](https://arxiv.org/html/2311.04235v2)\n- [Implementation Intentions Research](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/10463283.2020.1808936)\n- [Jesse Vincent's Superpowers Blog](https://blog.fsck.com/2025/10/09/superpowers/)\n- [Anthropic Best Practices for Skills](https://docs.anthropic.com/en/docs/agents-and-tools/agent-skills)"}],"stop_reason":"end_turn","stop_sequence":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":3,"cache_creation_input_tokens":36769,"cache_read_input_tokens":85948,"output_tokens":9259,"server_tool_use":{"web_search_requests":0,"web_fetch_requests":0},"service_tier":"standard","cache_creation":{"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":0,"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":36769},"inference_geo":"","iterations":[],"speed":"standard"}},"requestId":"req_011CZERe3Q3GBiv4tdHHLqzQ","type":"assistant","uuid":"39f8ca67-1c9a-452d-b334-8888bed89366","timestamp":"2026-03-20T13:43:20.570Z","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir","sessionId":"0c5a9bfa-6816-42b1-a97d-37ae7dad26c5","version":"2.1.80","gitBranch":"feat/remaining-6-fixes","slug":"mighty-wondering-giraffe"}
